<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="background:transparent;border-top:1px solid #aaa; text-align:center;">Techniques</div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content">

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content">
<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="background:transparent;border-top:1px solid #aaa; text-align:center;">Types</div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Abstract_art" title="Abstract art">Abstract</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Art_history" title="Art history">Art history</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Figurative_art" title="Figurative art">Figurative</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Funerary_art" title="Funerary art">Funerary</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Na%C3%AFve_art" title="Naïve art">Naïve</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Narrative_art" title="Narrative art">Narrative</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Realism_(arts)" title="Realism (arts)">Naturalist</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Symbolism_(arts)" title="Symbolism (arts)">Symbolic</a></li></ul></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_art_sidebar" title="Template:History of art sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_art_sidebar" title="Template talk:History of art sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_art_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/History_of_art" title="History of art">history of art</a>, <b>prehistoric art</b> is all art produced in preliterate, <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistorical</a> cultures beginning somewhere in very late geological history, and generally continuing until that culture either develops writing or other methods of record-keeping, or makes significant contact with another culture that has, and that makes some record of major historical events. At this point <a href="/wiki/Ancient_art" title="Ancient art">ancient art</a> begins, for the older literate cultures. The end-date for what is covered by the term thus varies greatly between different parts of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[a]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest human artifacts showing evidence of workmanship with an artistic purpose are the subject of some debate. It is clear that such workmanship existed by 40,000 years ago in the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a> era, although it is quite possible that it began earlier. In September 2018, scientists reported the discovery of the earliest known drawing by <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i>, which is estimated to be 73,000 years old, much earlier than the 43,000 years old artifacts understood to be the earliest known modern human drawings found previously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20180912_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20180912-3">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Engraved shells created by <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> dating as far back as 500,000 years ago<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup> have been found, although experts disagree on whether these engravings can be properly classified as 'art'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> From the Upper Paleolithic through to the <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cave_painting" title="Cave painting">cave paintings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portable_art" title="Portable art">portable art</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Figurine" title="Figurine">figurines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bead" title="Bead">beads</a> predominated, with decorative figured workings also seen on some utilitarian objects. In the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> evidence of early <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a> appeared, as did <a href="/wiki/Sculpture" title="Sculpture">sculpture</a> and the construction of <a href="/wiki/Megalith" title="Megalith">megaliths</a>. Early <a href="/wiki/Rock_art" title="Rock art">rock art</a> also first appeared during this period. The advent of <a href="/wiki/Metalworking" title="Metalworking">metalworking</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> brought additional media available for use in making art, an increase in stylistic diversity, and the creation of objects that did not have any obvious function other than art. It also saw the development in some areas of artisans, a class of people specializing in the production of art, as well as <a href="/wiki/History_of_writing" title="History of writing">early writing systems</a>. By the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>, civilizations with writing had arisen from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a> to <a href="/wiki/History_of_China#Ancient_China" title="History of China">Ancient China</a>.
</p><p>Many indigenous peoples from around the world continued to produce artistic works distinctive to their geographic area and culture, until exploration and commerce brought record-keeping methods to them. Some cultures, notably the <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Maya civilization</a>, independently developed writing during the time they flourished, which was then later lost. These cultures may be classified as prehistoric, especially if their writing systems have not been deciphered.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Paleolithic_era">Paleolithic era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Paleolithic era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Stone_Age_art" title="List of Stone Age art">List of Stone Age art</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lower_and_Middle_Paleolithic">Lower and Middle Paleolithic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Lower and Middle Paleolithic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Art_of_the_Middle_Paleolithic" title="Art of the Middle Paleolithic">Art of the Middle Paleolithic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Homo_Erectus_shell_with_geometric_incisions_circa_500,000_BP,_Naturalis_Biodiversity_Center,_Netherlands_(with_detail).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="878" data-file-width="3083" decoding="async" height="83" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Homo_Erectus_shell_with_geometric_incisions_circa_500%2C000_BP%2C_Naturalis_Biodiversity_Center%2C_Netherlands_%28with_detail%29.jpg/290px-Homo_Erectus_shell_with_geometric_incisions_circa_500%2C000_BP%2C_Naturalis_Biodiversity_Center%2C_Netherlands_%28with_detail%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Homo_Erectus_shell_with_geometric_incisions_circa_500%2C000_BP%2C_Naturalis_Biodiversity_Center%2C_Netherlands_%28with_detail%29.jpg/435px-Homo_Erectus_shell_with_geometric_incisions_circa_500%2C000_BP%2C_Naturalis_Biodiversity_Center%2C_Netherlands_%28with_detail%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Homo_Erectus_shell_with_geometric_incisions_circa_500%2C000_BP%2C_Naturalis_Biodiversity_Center%2C_Netherlands_%28with_detail%29.jpg/580px-Homo_Erectus_shell_with_geometric_incisions_circa_500%2C000_BP%2C_Naturalis_Biodiversity_Center%2C_Netherlands_%28with_detail%29.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Homo_Erectus_shell_with_geometric_incisions_circa_500,000_BP,_Naturalis_Biodiversity_Center,_Netherlands_(with_detail).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pseudodon_shell_DUB1006-fL" title="Pseudodon shell DUB1006-fL">Pseudodon shell DUB1006-fL</a> with the earliest known geometric engravings, supposedly, made by <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i>; ca. 500,000 BP; from <a href="/wiki/Trinil" title="Trinil">Trinil</a> (<a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>); <a href="/wiki/Naturalis_Biodiversity_Center" title="Naturalis Biodiversity Center">Naturalis Biodiversity Center</a> (<a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joordens2015_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Joordens2015-6">[5]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The earliest undisputed art originated with the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Aurignacian" title="Aurignacian">Aurignacian</a> <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_culture" title="Archaeological culture">archaeological culture</a> in the Upper Paleolithic. However, there is some evidence that the preference for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aesthetic" title="Aesthetic">aesthetic</a> emerged in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic" title="Middle Paleolithic">Middle Paleolithic</a>, from 100,000 to 50,000 years ago. Some archaeologists have interpreted certain Middle Paleolithic artifacts as early examples of artistic expression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup>
The symmetry of artifacts, evidence of attention to the detail of tool shape, has led some investigators to conceive of <a href="/wiki/Acheulean" title="Acheulean">Acheulean</a> <a href="/wiki/Hand_axe" title="Hand axe">hand axes</a> and especially laurel points as having been produced with a degree of artistic expression.
</p><p>Similarly, a <a href="/wiki/Zigzag" title="Zigzag">zigzag</a> engraving supposedly made with a <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">shark</a> tooth on a freshwater <a href="/wiki/Pseudodon_shell_DUB1006-fL" title="Pseudodon shell DUB1006-fL">Pseudodon shell DUB1006-fL</a> around 500,000 years ago (i.e. well into the <a href="/wiki/Lower_Paleolithic" title="Lower Paleolithic">Lower Paleolithic</a>), associated with <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i>, could be the earliest evidence of artistic activity, but the actual intent behind this geometric ornament is not known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joordens2015_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Joordens2015-6">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Claimed_Oldest_Known_Drawing_by_Human_Hands_Discovered_in_South_African_Cave.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="288" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Claimed_Oldest_Known_Drawing_by_Human_Hands_Discovered_in_South_African_Cave.jpg/220px-Claimed_Oldest_Known_Drawing_by_Human_Hands_Discovered_in_South_African_Cave.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Claimed_Oldest_Known_Drawing_by_Human_Hands_Discovered_in_South_African_Cave.jpg/330px-Claimed_Oldest_Known_Drawing_by_Human_Hands_Discovered_in_South_African_Cave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Claimed_Oldest_Known_Drawing_by_Human_Hands_Discovered_in_South_African_Cave.jpg/440px-Claimed_Oldest_Known_Drawing_by_Human_Hands_Discovered_in_South_African_Cave.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Claimed_Oldest_Known_Drawing_by_Human_Hands_Discovered_in_South_African_Cave.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Claimed "Oldest known drawing by human hands", discovered in <a href="/wiki/Blombos_Cave" title="Blombos Cave">Blombos Cave</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>. Estimated to be 73,000 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20180912_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20180912-3">[2]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>There are other claims of Middle Paleolithic sculpture, dubbed the "<a href="/wiki/Venus_of_Tan-Tan" title="Venus of Tan-Tan">Venus of Tan-Tan</a>" (before 300 kya)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChase2005145–146_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChase2005145–146-9">[8]</a></sup> and the "<a href="/wiki/Venus_of_Berekhat_Ram" title="Venus of Berekhat Ram">Venus of Berekhat Ram</a>" (250 kya). In 2002 in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blombos_cave" title="Blombos cave">Blombos cave</a>, situated in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, stones were discovered engraved with grid or cross-hatch patterns, dated to some 70,000 years ago. This suggested to some researchers that <a href="/wiki/Human#Paleolithic" title="Human">early <i>Homo sapiens</i></a> were capable of abstraction and production of abstract art or symbolic art. Several archaeologists including <a href="/wiki/Richard_Klein_(paleoanthropologist)" title="Richard Klein (paleoanthropologist)">Richard Klein</a> are hesitant to accept the Blombos caves as the first example of actual art.
</p><p>In September 2018 the discovery in South Africa of the earliest known drawing by <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> was announced, which is estimated to be 73,000 years old, much earlier than the 43,000 years old artifacts understood to be the earliest known modern human drawings found previously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20180912_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20180912-3">[2]</a></sup> The drawing shows a crosshatched pattern made up of nine fine lines. The sudden termination of all of the lines on the fragment edges indicate that the pattern originally extended over a larger surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> It is also estimated that the pattern was most likely more complex and structured in its entirety than shown on the discovered area. Initially, when this drawing was found, there was much debate. To prove that this drawing was created by Homo Sapiens, French team members who specialized in chemical analysis of pigments, reproduced the same lines using a variety of techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> They concluded that the lines making up the drawing were intentional and were most likely made with ocher. This discovery adds further dimensions to understanding the behavior and cognition of early homo sapiens.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neanderthals" title="Neanderthals">Neanderthals</a> <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal_behavior#Claims_of_art_and_adornment" title="Neanderthal behavior">may have made art</a>. Painted designs in the caves of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La_Pasiega" title="La Pasiega">La Pasiega</a> (<a href="/wiki/Cantabria" title="Cantabria">Cantabria</a>), a hand stencil in <a href="/wiki/Cave_of_Maltravieso" title="Cave of Maltravieso">Maltravieso</a> (<a href="/wiki/Extremadura" title="Extremadura">Extremadura</a>), and red-painted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Speleothems" title="Speleothems">speleothems</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ardales" title="Ardales">Ardales</a> (<a href="/wiki/Andalusia" title="Andalusia">Andalusia</a>) are dated to 64,800 years ago, predating by at least 20,000 years the arrival of modern humans in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffmann2018_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffmann2018-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> In July 2021, scientists reported the discovery of a <a href="/wiki/Bone_carving" title="Bone carving">bone carving</a>, one of the world's <a href="/wiki/Art_of_the_Upper_Paleolithic" title="Art of the Upper Paleolithic">oldest works of art</a>, made by Neanderthals about 51,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SA-20210706_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-SA-20210706-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEE-20210705_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-NEE-20210705-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Upper_Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Upper Paleolithic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Art_of_the_Upper_Paleolithic" title="Art of the Upper Paleolithic">Art of the Upper Paleolithic</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:156px;max-width:156px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:97px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lubang_Jeriji_Sal%C3%A9h_cave_painting_of_Bull.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1155" data-file-width="1814" decoding="async" height="98" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Lubang_Jeriji_Sal%C3%A9h_cave_painting_of_Bull.jpg/154px-Lubang_Jeriji_Sal%C3%A9h_cave_painting_of_Bull.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Lubang_Jeriji_Sal%C3%A9h_cave_painting_of_Bull.jpg/231px-Lubang_Jeriji_Sal%C3%A9h_cave_painting_of_Bull.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Lubang_Jeriji_Sal%C3%A9h_cave_painting_of_Bull.jpg/308px-Lubang_Jeriji_Sal%C3%A9h_cave_painting_of_Bull.jpg 2x" width="154"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:132px;max-width:132px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:97px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lascaux_04.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="201" data-file-width="267" decoding="async" height="98" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lascaux_04.jpg/130px-Lascaux_04.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lascaux_04.jpg/195px-Lascaux_04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lascaux_04.jpg/260px-Lascaux_04.jpg 2x" width="130"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">* (Left) Probably the oldest known painting, from the cave of <a href="/wiki/Lubang_Jeriji_Sal%C3%A9h" title="Lubang Jeriji Saléh">Lubang Jeriji Saléh</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesian</a> island of <a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a>, circa 40,000 BC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20181107-cz_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20181107-cz-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20181107_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20181107-17">[16]</a></sup>
<ul><li>(Right) <a href="/wiki/Aurochs" title="Aurochs">Aurochs</a> on a cave painting in <a href="/wiki/Lascaux" title="Lascaux">Lascaux</a>, France</li></ul></div></div></div></div>
<p>In November 2018, scientists reported the discovery of the oldest known <a href="/wiki/Figurative_art" title="Figurative art">figurative art painting</a>, over 40,000 (perhaps as old as 52,000) years old, of an unknown animal, in the cave of <a href="/wiki/Lubang_Jeriji_Sal%C3%A9h" title="Lubang Jeriji Saléh">Lubang Jeriji Saléh</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesian</a> island of <a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20181107-cz_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20181107-cz-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20181107_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20181107-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some of the oldest undisputed works of figurative art were found in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schw%C3%A4bische_Alb" title="Schwäbische Alb">Schwäbische Alb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baden-W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Baden-Württemberg">Baden-Württemberg</a>, Germany. The earliest of these, the <a href="/wiki/Venus_figurine" title="Venus figurine">Venus figurine</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Venus_of_Hohle_Fels" title="Venus of Hohle Fels">Venus of Hohle Fels</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lion-man" title="Lion-man">Lion-man figurine</a>, date to some 40,000 years ago.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venus_of_Willendorf_frontview_retouched_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2220" data-file-width="1350" decoding="async" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Venus_of_Willendorf_frontview_retouched_2.jpg/170px-Venus_of_Willendorf_frontview_retouched_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Venus_of_Willendorf_frontview_retouched_2.jpg/255px-Venus_of_Willendorf_frontview_retouched_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Venus_of_Willendorf_frontview_retouched_2.jpg/340px-Venus_of_Willendorf_frontview_retouched_2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venus_of_Willendorf_frontview_retouched_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Venus_of_Willendorf" title="Venus of Willendorf">Venus of Willendorf</a></div></div></div>
<p>Further depictional art from the Upper Palaeolithic period (broadly 40,000 to 10,000 years ago) includes <a href="/wiki/Cave_painting" title="Cave painting">cave painting</a> (e.g., those at <a href="/wiki/Chauvet_Cave" title="Chauvet Cave">Chauvet</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Altamira_(cave)" title="Altamira (cave)">Altamira</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pech_Merle" title="Pech Merle">Pech Merle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caves_of_Arcy-sur-Cure" title="Caves of Arcy-sur-Cure">Arcy-sur-Cure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lascaux" title="Lascaux">Lascaux</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Portable_art" title="Portable art">portable art</a>: Venus figurines like the <a href="/wiki/Venus_of_Willendorf" title="Venus of Willendorf">Venus of Willendorf</a>, as well as animal carvings like the <a href="/wiki/Swimming_Reindeer" title="Swimming Reindeer">Swimming Reindeer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wolverine_pendant_of_Les_Eyzies" title="Wolverine pendant of Les Eyzies">Wolverine pendant of Les Eyzies</a>, and several of the objects known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B%C3%A2tons_de_commandement" title="Bâtons de commandement">bâtons de commandement</a>.
</p><p>Paintings in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pettakere_cave" title="Pettakere cave">Pettakere cave</a> on the Indonesian island of <a href="/wiki/Sulawesi" title="Sulawesi">Sulawesi</a> are up to 40,000 years old, a similar date to the oldest European cave art, which may suggest an older common origin for this type of art, perhaps in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Monumental open-air art in Europe from this period includes the rock-art at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C%C3%B4a_Valley_Paleolithic_Art" title="Côa Valley Paleolithic Art">Côa Valley</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mazouco&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mazouco (page does not exist)">Mazouco</a> in Portugal, <a href="/wiki/Domingo_Garc%C3%ADa,_Segovia" title="Domingo García, Segovia">Domingo García</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siega_Verde" title="Siega Verde">Siega Verde</a> in Spain, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rocher_grav%C3%A9_de_Fornols&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rocher gravé de Fornols (page does not exist)">Rocher gravé de Fornols</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rocher_grav%C3%A9_de_Fornols" title="fr:Rocher gravé de Fornols">fr</a>]</span> in France.
</p><p>A cave at <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Turobong_cave&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Turobong cave (page does not exist)">Turobong</a> in South Korea containing human remains has been found to contain carved deer bones and depictions of deer that may be as much as 40,000 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPortal200025_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPortal200025-19">[18]</a></sup> Petroglyphs of deer or reindeer found at Sokchang-ri may also date to the Upper Paleolithic. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sherd" title="Sherd">Potsherds</a> in a style reminiscent of early Japanese work have been found at Kosan-ri on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jeju_island" title="Jeju island">Jeju island</a>, which, due to lower sea levels at the time, would have been accessible from Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPortal200026_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPortal200026-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oldest <a href="/wiki/Petroglyph" title="Petroglyph">petroglyphs</a> are dated to approximately the Mesolithic and late <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a> boundary, about 10,000 to 12,000 years ago.
The earliest undisputed <a href="/wiki/Rock_art#Africa" title="Rock art">African rock art</a> dates back about 10,000 years. The first naturalistic paintings of humans found in Africa date back about 8,000 years apparently originating in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_River" title="Nile River">Nile River</a> valley, spread as far west as Mali about 10,000 years ago.
Noted sites containing early art include <a href="/wiki/Tassili_n%27Ajjer" title="Tassili n'Ajjer">Tassili n'Ajjer</a> in southern Algeria, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tadrart_Acacus" title="Tadrart Acacus">Tadrart Acacus</a> in Libya (A Unesco World Heritage site), and the <a href="/wiki/Tibesti_Mountains" title="Tibesti Mountains">Tibesti Mountains</a> in northern Chad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoulsonCampbell2001150–155_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoulsonCampbell2001150–155-21">[20]</a></sup> Rock carvings at the <a href="/wiki/Wonderwerk_Cave" title="Wonderwerk Cave">Wonderwerk Cave</a> in South Africa have been dated to this age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThackerayThackerayBeaumontVogel1981_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThackerayThackerayBeaumontVogel1981-22">[21]</a></sup> Contentious dates as far back as 29,000 years have been obtained at a site in Tanzania. A site at the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11_Cave" title="Apollo 11 Cave">Apollo 11 Cave</a> complex in Namibia has been dated to 27,000 years.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6bekli_Tepe" title="Göbekli Tepe">Göbekli Tepe</a> in Turkey has circles of massive T-shaped stone pillars dating back to the 10th–8th millennium BCE; the world's oldest known megaliths. Many of the pillars are decorated with abstract, enigmatic pictograms and carved animal reliefs.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_art_history" title="Eastern art history">Eastern art history</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Chinese_art" title="History of Chinese art">History of Chinese art</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dancing_girl_of_Mohenjo-daro.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3518" data-file-width="2257" decoding="async" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Dancing_girl_of_Mohenjo-daro.jpg/170px-Dancing_girl_of_Mohenjo-daro.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Dancing_girl_of_Mohenjo-daro.jpg/255px-Dancing_girl_of_Mohenjo-daro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Dancing_girl_of_Mohenjo-daro.jpg/340px-Dancing_girl_of_Mohenjo-daro.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dancing_girl_of_Mohenjo-daro.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dancing_Girl_(Mohenjo-daro)" title="Dancing Girl (Mohenjo-daro)">Dancing Girl</a></i> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilization" title="Indus Valley civilization">Indus Valley civilization</a></div></div></div>
<p>Asia was the cradle for several significant civilizations, most notably those of China and South Asia. The prehistory of eastern Asia is especially interesting, as the relatively early introduction of writing and historical record-keeping in China has a notable impact on the immediately surrounding cultures and geographic areas. Little of the very rich traditions of the <a href="/wiki/Art_of_Mesopotamia" title="Art of Mesopotamia">art of Mesopotamia</a> counts as prehistoric, as writing was introduced so early there, but neighbouring cultures such as <a href="/wiki/Urartu" title="Urartu">Urartu</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luristan" title="Luristan">Luristan</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a> had significant and complex artistic traditions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_sub-continent">Indian sub-continent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Indian sub-continent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The earliest Indian paintings were the rock paintings of <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistoric</a> times, the <a href="/wiki/Petroglyph" title="Petroglyph">petroglyphs</a> as found in places like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_Shelters_of_Bhimbetka" title="Rock Shelters of Bhimbetka">Rock Shelters of Bhimbetka</a>, and some of them are dated to circa 8,000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilization" title="Indus Valley civilization">Indus Valley civilization</a> produced fine small <a href="/wiki/Stamp_seal" title="Stamp seal">stamp seals</a> and sculptures, and may have been literate, but after its collapse there are relatively few artistic remains until the literate period, probably as perishable materials were used.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Azerbaijan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gobustan_National_Park" title="Gobustan National Park">Gobustan National Park</a> reserve located at the south-east of the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Caucasus" title="Greater Caucasus">Greater Caucasus Mountains</a> in Azerbaijan, 60 km away from <a href="/wiki/Baku" title="Baku">Baku</a> date back more than 12 thousand years ago. The reserve has more than 6,000 rock carvings depicting mostly hunting scenes, human and animal figures. There are also longship illustrations similar to <a href="/wiki/Viking_ships" title="Viking ships">Viking ships</a>. Gobustan is also characterized by its natural musical stone called Gavaldash (tambourine stone).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gold_Mask_(%E9%BB%84%E9%87%91%E9%9D%A2%E7%BD%A9).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2640" data-file-width="1980" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Gold_Mask_%28%E9%BB%84%E9%87%91%E9%9D%A2%E7%BD%A9%29.jpg/170px-Gold_Mask_%28%E9%BB%84%E9%87%91%E9%9D%A2%E7%BD%A9%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Gold_Mask_%28%E9%BB%84%E9%87%91%E9%9D%A2%E7%BD%A9%29.jpg/255px-Gold_Mask_%28%E9%BB%84%E9%87%91%E9%9D%A2%E7%BD%A9%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Gold_Mask_%28%E9%BB%84%E9%87%91%E9%9D%A2%E7%BD%A9%29.jpg/340px-Gold_Mask_%28%E9%BB%84%E9%87%91%E9%9D%A2%E7%BD%A9%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gold_Mask_(%E9%BB%84%E9%87%91%E9%9D%A2%E7%BD%A9).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two bronze heads from <a href="/wiki/Sanxingdui" title="Sanxingdui">Sanxingdui</a>, covered with <a href="/wiki/Gold_leaf" title="Gold leaf">gold leaf</a></div></div></div>
<p>Prehistoric artwork such as painted pottery in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neolithic_China" title="Neolithic China">Neolithic China</a> can be traced back to the <a href="/wiki/Yangshao_culture" title="Yangshao culture">Yangshao culture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Longshan_culture" title="Longshan culture">Longshan culture</a> of the Yellow River valley. During China's <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>, Chinese of the ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shang_Dynasty" title="Shang Dynasty">Shang Dynasty</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhou_Dynasty" title="Zhou Dynasty">Zhou Dynasty</a> produced multitudes of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_ritual_bronzes" title="Chinese ritual bronzes">Chinese ritual bronzes</a>, which are elaborate versions of ordinary vessels and other objects used in rituals of <a href="/wiki/Ancestor_veneration_in_China" title="Ancestor veneration in China">ancestor veneration</a>, decorated with <i><a href="/wiki/Taotie" title="Taotie">taotie</a></i> motifs and by the late Shang <a href="/wiki/Chinese_bronze_inscriptions" title="Chinese bronze inscriptions">Chinese bronze inscriptions</a>. Discoveries in 1987 in <a href="/wiki/Sanxingdui" title="Sanxingdui">Sanxingdui</a> in central China revealed a previously unknown pre-literate Bronze Age culture whose artefacts included spectacular very large bronze figures (example left), and which appeared culturally very different from the contemporary late Shang, which has always formed part of the account of the continuous tradition of Chinese culture.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Prehistoric_Art" title="Japanese Prehistoric Art">Japanese Prehistoric Art</a></div>
<p>According to archeological evidence, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dmon" title="Jōmon">Jōmon</a> people in ancient Japan were among the first to develop <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jomon_pottery" title="Jomon pottery">pottery</a>, dated from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/11th_millennium_BCE" title="11th millennium BCE">11th millennium BCE</a>. With growing sophistication, the Jōmon created patterns by impressing the wet clay with braided or unbraided cord and sticks.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Korea">Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Korean_art" title="Korean art">Korean art</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Korea" title="Prehistoric Korea">Prehistoric Korea</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_archaeological_periods_(Korea)" title="List of archaeological periods (Korea)">List of archaeological periods (Korea)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Korea-Neolithic.age-Pot-01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Korea-Neolithic.age-Pot-01.jpg/220px-Korea-Neolithic.age-Pot-01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Korea-Neolithic.age-Pot-01.jpg/330px-Korea-Neolithic.age-Pot-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Korea-Neolithic.age-Pot-01.jpg/440px-Korea-Neolithic.age-Pot-01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Korea-Neolithic.age-Pot-01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Korean Neolithic pot found in Busan, 3500 BCE</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest examples of Korean art consist of Stone Age works dating from 3000 BCE. These mainly consist of <a href="/wiki/Votive_offering" title="Votive offering">votive sculptures</a>, although petroglyphs have also been recently rediscovered. <a href="/wiki/Rock_art" title="Rock art">Rock arts</a>, elaborate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stone_tools" title="Stone tools">stone tools</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">potteries</a> were also prevalent.
</p><p>This early period was followed by the art styles of various Korean kingdoms and dynasties. In these periods, artists often adopted Chinese style in their artworks. However, Koreans not only adopted but also modified Chinese culture with a native preference for simple elegance, purity of nature and spontaneity. This filtering of Chinese styles later influenced Japanese artistic traditions, due to cultural and geographical circumstances.
</p><p>The prehistory of Korean ends with the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea" title="Three Kingdoms of Korea">Three Kingdoms of Korea</a>, which are documented in the <a href="/wiki/Samguk_Sagi" title="Samguk Sagi">Samguk Sagi</a>, a 12th-century CE text written in <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">Classical Chinese</a> (the written language of the literati in traditional Korea), as beginning in the 1st century BCE; some mention of earlier history is also made in Chinese texts, like the 3rd-century CE <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanguo_Zhi" title="Sanguo Zhi">Sanguo Zhi</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jeulmun_period">Jeulmun period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Jeulmun period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Jeulmun_pottery_period" title="Jeulmun pottery period">Jeulmun pottery period</a></div>
<p>Clearer evidence of culture emerges in the late Neolithic, known in Korea as the <a href="/wiki/Jeulmun_pottery_period" title="Jeulmun pottery period">Jeulmun pottery period</a>, with pottery similar to that found in the adjacent regions of China, decorated with Z-shaped patterns. The earliest Neolithic sites with pottery remains, for example <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Osan-ri&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Osan-ri (page does not exist)">Osan-ri</a>, date to 6000–4500 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPortal200026_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPortal200026-20">[19]</a></sup> This pottery is characterized by comb patterning, with the pot frequently having a pointed base. Ornaments from this time include masks made of shell, with notable finds at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dongsam-dong_Shell_Midden" title="Dongsam-dong Shell Midden">Tongsam-dong</a>, Osan-ri, and Sinam-ri. Hand-shaped clay figurines have been found at Nongpo-dong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPortal200027_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPortal200027-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mumun_period">Mumun period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Mumun period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mumun_pottery_period" title="Mumun pottery period">Mumun pottery period</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Korea-Bronze.age-Jinju-Pot-01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Korea-Bronze.age-Jinju-Pot-01.jpg/220px-Korea-Bronze.age-Jinju-Pot-01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Korea-Bronze.age-Jinju-Pot-01.jpg/330px-Korea-Bronze.age-Jinju-Pot-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Korea-Bronze.age-Jinju-Pot-01.jpg/440px-Korea-Bronze.age-Jinju-Pot-01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Korea-Bronze.age-Jinju-Pot-01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Large Middle Mumun (c. 800 BCE) storage vessel unearthed from a pit-house in or near <a href="/wiki/Daepyeong" title="Daepyeong">Daepyeong</a></div></div></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Mumun_pottery_period" title="Mumun pottery period">Mumun pottery period</a>, roughly between 1500 BCE and 300 BCE, agriculture expanded, and evidence of larger-scale political structures became apparent, as villages grew and some burials became more elaborate. Megalithic tombs and <a href="/wiki/Dolmen" title="Dolmen">dolmens</a> throughout Korea date to this time. The pottery of the time is in a distinctive undecorated style. Many of these changes in style may have occurred due to immigration of new peoples from the north, although this is a subject of debate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPortal200029_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPortal200029-33">[32]</a></sup> At a number of sites in southern Korea there are rock art panels that are thought to date from this period, mainly for stylistic reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPortal200033_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPortal200033-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>While the exact date of the introduction of bronzework into Korea is also a matter of debate, it is clear that bronze was being worked by about 700 BCE. Finds include stylistically distinctive daggers, mirrors, and belt buckles, with evidence by the 1st century BCE of a widespread, locally distinctive, bronzeworking culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPortal200034–35_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPortal200034–35-35">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Protohistoric_Korea">Protohistoric Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Protohistoric Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Goguryeo_tomb_mural.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="611" data-file-width="740" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Goguryeo_tomb_mural.jpg/220px-Goguryeo_tomb_mural.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Goguryeo_tomb_mural.jpg/330px-Goguryeo_tomb_mural.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Goguryeo_tomb_mural.jpg/440px-Goguryeo_tomb_mural.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Goguryeo_tomb_mural.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goguryeo_tomb_mural" title="Goguryeo tomb mural">Goguryeo tomb mural</a></div></div></div>
<p>The time between 300 BCE and the founding and stabilization of the Three Kingdoms around 300 CE is characterized artistically and archaeologically by increasing trade with China and Japan, something that Chinese histories of the time corroborate. The expansionist Chinese invaded and established <a href="/wiki/Commandery_(China)" title="Commandery (China)">commanderies</a> in northern Korea as early as the 1st century BCE; they were driven out by the 4th century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPortal200038_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPortal200038-36">[35]</a></sup> The remains of some of these, especially that of <a href="/wiki/Lelang_Commandery" title="Lelang Commandery">Lelang</a>, near modern <a href="/wiki/Pyongyang" title="Pyongyang">Pyongyang</a>, have yielded many artifacts in a typical Han style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPortal200039_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPortal200039-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chinese histories also record the beginnings of iron works in Korea in the 1st century BCE. Stoneware and <a href="/wiki/Kiln" title="Kiln">kiln</a>-fired pottery also appears to date from this time, although there is controversy over the dates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPortal200040_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPortal200040-38">[37]</a></sup> Pottery of distinctly Japanese origin is found in Korea, and metalwork of Korean origin is found in northeastern China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPortal200041_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPortal200041-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Steppes_Art">Steppes Art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Steppes Art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Placca_pantera,_da_regione_di_krasnodar,_kurgan_chertomlyk,_oro_a_sbalzo_e_cesellato,_fine_VII_sec_ac..JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="1555" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Placca_pantera%2C_da_regione_di_krasnodar%2C_kurgan_chertomlyk%2C_oro_a_sbalzo_e_cesellato%2C_fine_VII_sec_ac..JPG/220px-Placca_pantera%2C_da_regione_di_krasnodar%2C_kurgan_chertomlyk%2C_oro_a_sbalzo_e_cesellato%2C_fine_VII_sec_ac..JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Placca_pantera%2C_da_regione_di_krasnodar%2C_kurgan_chertomlyk%2C_oro_a_sbalzo_e_cesellato%2C_fine_VII_sec_ac..JPG/330px-Placca_pantera%2C_da_regione_di_krasnodar%2C_kurgan_chertomlyk%2C_oro_a_sbalzo_e_cesellato%2C_fine_VII_sec_ac..JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Placca_pantera%2C_da_regione_di_krasnodar%2C_kurgan_chertomlyk%2C_oro_a_sbalzo_e_cesellato%2C_fine_VII_sec_ac..JPG/440px-Placca_pantera%2C_da_regione_di_krasnodar%2C_kurgan_chertomlyk%2C_oro_a_sbalzo_e_cesellato%2C_fine_VII_sec_ac..JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Placca_pantera,_da_regione_di_krasnodar,_kurgan_chertomlyk,_oro_a_sbalzo_e_cesellato,_fine_VII_sec_ac..JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Late 7th-century <a href="/wiki/Scythia" title="Scythia">Scythian</a> plaque of a leopard</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scythian_art" title="Scythian art">Scythian art</a></div>
<p>Superb samples of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steppes_art" title="Steppes art">Steppes art</a> – mostly golden jewellery and trappings for horse – are found over a vast expanses of land stretching from <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> to <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>. Dating from the period between the 7th and 3rd centuries BCE, the objects are usually diminutive, as may be expected from nomadic people always on the move. Art of the steppes is primarily an <a href="/wiki/Animal_style" title="Animal style">animal art</a>, i.e., combat scenes involving several animals (real or imaginary) or single animal figures (such as golden stags) predominate. The best known of the various peoples involved are the <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a>, at the European end of the steppe, who were especially likely to bury gold items.
</p><p>Among the most famous finds was made in 1947, when the Soviet archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Rudenko" title="Sergei Rudenko">Sergei Rudenko</a> discovered a royal burial at <a href="/wiki/Pazyryk_burials" title="Pazyryk burials">Pazyryk</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Altay_Mountains" title="Altay Mountains">Altay Mountains</a>, which featured – among many other important objects – the most ancient extant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pile_rug" title="Pile rug">pile rug</a>, probably made in Persia. Unusually for prehistoric burials, those in the northern parts of the area may preserve organic materials such as wood and textiles that normally would decay. Steppes people both gave and took influences from neighbouring cultures from Europe to China, and later Scythian pieces are heavily influenced by ancient Greek style, and probably often made by Greeks in Scythia.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Near_East">Near East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Near East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gobekli_Tepe_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4288" data-file-width="2848" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Gobekli_Tepe_2.jpg/170px-Gobekli_Tepe_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Gobekli_Tepe_2.jpg/255px-Gobekli_Tepe_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Gobekli_Tepe_2.jpg/340px-Gobekli_Tepe_2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gobekli_Tepe_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pillar from <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6bekli_Tepe" title="Göbekli Tepe">Göbekli Tepe</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_relief" title="Low relief">low reliefs</a> of what are believed to be a <a href="/wiki/Bull" title="Bull">bull</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fox" title="Fox">fox</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Crane_(bird)" title="Crane (bird)">crane</a>, 9600 to 8800 BCE.</div></div></div>
<p>The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ain_Sakhri_Lovers" title="Ain Sakhri Lovers">Ain Sakhri Lovers</a></i> from modern <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, is a small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natufian" title="Natufian">Natufian</a> carving in <a href="/wiki/Calcite" title="Calcite">calcite</a>, from about 9,000 BCE. Around the same time, the extraordinary site of <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6bekli_Tepe" title="Göbekli Tepe">Göbekli Tepe</a> in eastern <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> was begun. During the first phase, belonging to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-pottery_Neolithic_A" title="Pre-pottery Neolithic A">Pre-Pottery Neolithic A</a> (PPNA), circles of massive but neatly shaped T-shaped stone pillars were erected – the world's oldest known <a href="/wiki/Megalith" title="Megalith">megaliths</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> More than 200 pillars in about 20 circles are currently known through <a href="/wiki/Geophysical_survey_(archaeology)" title="Geophysical survey (archaeology)">geophysical surveys</a>. Each pillar has a height of up to 6 m (20 ft) and weighs up to 10 tons. They are fitted into sockets that were hewn out of the <a href="/wiki/Bedrock" title="Bedrock">bedrock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> In the second phase, belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Pottery_Neolithic_B" title="Pre-Pottery Neolithic B">Pre-Pottery Neolithic B</a> (PPNB), the erected pillars are smaller and stood in rectangular rooms with floors of polished <a href="/wiki/Lime_(material)" title="Lime (material)">lime</a>. On the smoothed surfaces of the pillars there are <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">reliefs</a> of animals, abstract patterns, and some human figures.
</p><p>By convention, prehistory in the Near East is taken to continue until the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> in the 6th century BCE, although writing existed in the region from nearly 2,000 years earlier. On that basis the very rich and long tradition of the <a href="/wiki/Art_of_Mesopotamia" title="Art of Mesopotamia">art of Mesopotamia</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_sculpture" title="Assyrian sculpture">Assyrian sculpture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hittite_art" title="Hittite art">Hittite art</a> and many other traditions such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luristan_bronzes" title="Luristan bronzes">Luristan bronzes</a> all fall under prehistoric art, even if covered with texts extolling the ruler, as many Assyrian palace reliefs are.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stone_Age">Stone Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Stone Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prehistoric_Hand_Outline_Cosquer_Cave.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2544" data-file-width="1865" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Prehistoric_Hand_Outline_Cosquer_Cave.JPG/170px-Prehistoric_Hand_Outline_Cosquer_Cave.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Prehistoric_Hand_Outline_Cosquer_Cave.JPG/255px-Prehistoric_Hand_Outline_Cosquer_Cave.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Prehistoric_Hand_Outline_Cosquer_Cave.JPG/340px-Prehistoric_Hand_Outline_Cosquer_Cave.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prehistoric_Hand_Outline_Cosquer_Cave.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hand_stencil" title="Hand stencil">Hand stencil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cosquer_Cave" title="Cosquer Cave">Cosquer Cave</a>, France, c. 27,000 years old</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Art_of_the_Upper_Paleolithic" title="Art of the Upper Paleolithic">Art of the Upper Paleolithic</a> includes carvings on antler and bone, especially of animals, as well as the so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venus_figurines" title="Venus figurines">Venus figurines</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cave_paintings" title="Cave paintings">cave paintings</a>, discussed above. Despite a warmer climate, the <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> period undoubtedly shows a falling-off from the heights of the preceding period. <a href="/wiki/Rock_art" title="Rock art">Rock art</a> is found in Scandinavia and northern Russia, and around the Mediterranean in <a href="/wiki/Rock_art_of_the_Iberian_Mediterranean_Basin" title="Rock art of the Iberian Mediterranean Basin">eastern Spain</a> and the earliest of the <a href="/wiki/Rock_Drawings_in_Valcamonica" title="Rock Drawings in Valcamonica">Rock Drawings in Valcamonica</a> in northern Italy, but not in between these areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESandars196875–98_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESandars196875–98-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> Examples of portable art include painted pebbles from the <a href="/wiki/Azilian" title="Azilian">Azilian</a> culture which succeeded the <a href="/wiki/Magdalenian" title="Magdalenian">Magdalenian</a>, and patterns on utilitarian objects, like the paddles from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tybrind_Vig" title="Tybrind Vig">Tybrind Vig</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>. The Mesolithic statues of <a href="/wiki/Lepenski_Vir" title="Lepenski Vir">Lepenski Vir</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_Gate_(Danube)" title="Iron Gate (Danube)">Iron Gate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a> date to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/7th_millennium_BCE" title="7th millennium BCE">7th millennium BCE</a> and represent either humans or mixtures of humans and fish. Simple pottery began to develop in various places, even in the absence of farming.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Mesolithic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Compared to the preceding Upper Paleolithic and the following Neolithic, there is rather less surviving art from the Mesolithic. The <a href="/wiki/Rock_art_of_the_Iberian_Mediterranean_Basin" title="Rock art of the Iberian Mediterranean Basin">Rock art of the Iberian Mediterranean Basin</a>, which probably spreads across from the Upper Paleolithic, is a widespread phenomenon, much less well known than the cave-paintings of the Upper Paleolithic, with which it makes an interesting contrast. The sites are now mostly cliff faces in the open air, and the subjects are now mostly human rather than animal, with large groups of small figures; there are 45 figures at <a href="/wiki/Roca_dels_Moros" title="Roca dels Moros">Roca dels Moros</a>. Clothing is shown, and scenes of dancing, fighting, hunting and food-gathering. The figures are much smaller than the animals of Paleolithic art, and depicted much more schematically, though often in energetic poses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESandars196887–96_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESandars196887–96-44">[43]</a></sup> A few small engraved <a href="/wiki/Pendant" title="Pendant">pendants</a> with suspension holes and simple engraved designs are known, some from northern Europe in <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a>, and one from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Starr_Carr" title="Starr Carr">Starr Carr</a> in Britain in <a href="/wiki/Shale" title="Shale">shale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>The rock art in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urals" title="Urals">Urals</a> appears to show similar changes after the Paleolithic, and the wooden <a href="/wiki/Shigir_Idol" title="Shigir Idol">Shigir Idol</a> is a rare survival of what may well have been a very common material for sculpture. It is a plank of <a href="/wiki/Larch" title="Larch">larch</a> carved with geometric motifs, but topped with a human head. Now in fragments, it would apparently have been over 5 metres tall when made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Neolithic">Neolithic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Neolithic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blessington_lunula.BM.WG.31_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1711" data-file-width="1868" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Blessington_lunula.BM.WG.31_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Blessington_lunula.BM.WG.31_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Blessington_lunula.BM.WG.31_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Blessington_lunula.BM.WG.31_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Blessington_lunula.BM.WG.31_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Blessington_lunula.BM.WG.31_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blessington_lunula.BM.WG.31_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gold_lunula" title="Gold lunula">Gold lunula</a> from <a href="/wiki/Blessington" title="Blessington">Blessington</a>, Ireland, Late Neolithic/Early Bronze Age, c. 2400–2000 BC</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a>, many <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> cultures, like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linearbandkeramic" title="Linearbandkeramic">Linearbandkeramic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lengyel" title="Lengyel">Lengyel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vin%C4%8Da_culture" title="Vinča culture">Vinča</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> produced female (rarely male) and animal statues that can be called art, and elaborate pottery decoration in, for example, the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=%C5%BDeliesovce&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Želiesovce (page does not exist)">Želiesovce</a> and painted <a href="/wiki/Lengyel" title="Lengyel">Lengyel</a> style.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megalithic" title="Megalithic">Megalithic</a> (i.e., large stone) monuments are found in the Neolithic Era from <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a> to Portugal, through France, and across southern England to most of Wales and Ireland. They are also found in northern Germany and Poland, as well as in Egypt in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahara_desert" title="Sahara desert">Sahara desert</a> (at <a href="/wiki/Nabta_Playa" title="Nabta Playa">Nabta Playa</a> and other sites). The best preserved of all temples and the oldest free standing structures are the <a href="/wiki/Megalithic_Temples_of_Malta" title="Megalithic Temples of Malta">Megalithic Temples of Malta</a>. They start in the 5th millennium BC, though some authors speculate on Mesolithic roots. One of the best-known prehistoric sites is <a href="/wiki/Stonehenge" title="Stonehenge">Stonehenge</a>, part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stonehenge_World_Heritage_Site" title="Stonehenge World Heritage Site">Stonehenge World Heritage Site</a> which contains hundreds of monuments and archaeological sites. Monuments have been found throughout most of Western and Northern Europe, notably at <a href="/wiki/Carnac" title="Carnac">Carnac</a>, France.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Newgrange_Entrance_Stone.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Newgrange_Entrance_Stone.jpg/220px-Newgrange_Entrance_Stone.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Newgrange_Entrance_Stone.jpg/330px-Newgrange_Entrance_Stone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Newgrange_Entrance_Stone.jpg/440px-Newgrange_Entrance_Stone.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Newgrange_Entrance_Stone.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Entrance stone with megalithic art at Newgrange</div></div></div>
<p>The large mound tomb at <a href="/wiki/Newgrange" title="Newgrange">Newgrange</a>, Ireland, dating to around 3200 BC, has its entrance marked with a massive stone carved with a complex <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triple_spiral" title="Triple spiral">design of spirals</a>. The mound at nearby <a href="/wiki/Knowth" title="Knowth">Knowth</a> has large flat rocks with rock engravings on their vertical faces all around its circumference, for which various meanings have been suggested, including depictions of the local valley, and the oldest known image of the Moon. Many of these monuments were megalithic tombs, and archaeologists speculate that most have religious significance. Knowth is reputed to have approximately one third of all megalithic art in Western Europe.
</p><p>In the central Alps, the <a href="/wiki/Camunni" title="Camunni">Camunni</a> made some 350,000 petroglyphs: see <a href="/wiki/Rock_Drawings_in_Valcamonica" title="Rock Drawings in Valcamonica">Rock Drawings in Valcamonica</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bronze_Age">Bronze Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Bronze Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tanumshede_2005_rock_carvings_5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1232" data-file-width="1632" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Tanumshede_2005_rock_carvings_5.jpg/220px-Tanumshede_2005_rock_carvings_5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Tanumshede_2005_rock_carvings_5.jpg/330px-Tanumshede_2005_rock_carvings_5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Tanumshede_2005_rock_carvings_5.jpg/440px-Tanumshede_2005_rock_carvings_5.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tanumshede_2005_rock_carvings_5.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Three men performing a ritual, <a href="/wiki/Bohusl%C3%A4n" title="Bohuslän">Bohuslän</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a></div></div></div>
<p>During the 3rd millennium BCE, the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> began in Europe, bringing with it a new medium for art. The increased efficiency of bronze tools also meant an increase in productivity, which led to a surplus — the first step in the creation of a class of artisans. Because of the increased wealth of society, luxury goods began to be created, especially decorated weapons.
</p><p>Examples include ceremonial bronze helmets, ornamental ax-heads and swords, elaborate instruments such as <a href="/wiki/Lur" title="Lur">lurer</a>, and other ceremonial objects without a practical purpose, such as the oversize <a href="/wiki/Oxborough_Dirk" title="Oxborough Dirk">Oxborough Dirk</a>. Special objects were made in gold; many more gold objects have survived from Western and Central Europe than from the Iron Age, many mysterious and strange objects ranging from <a href="/wiki/Gold_lunula" title="Gold lunula">lunulas</a>, apparently an Irish speciality, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mold_Cape" title="Mold Cape">Mold Cape</a> and <a href="/wiki/Golden_hat" title="Golden hat">Golden hats</a>. Pottery from Central Europe can be elaborately shaped and decorated. <a href="/wiki/Rock_art" title="Rock art">Rock art</a>, showing scenes from the religious rituals have been found in many areas, for example in <a href="/wiki/Bohusl%C3%A4n" title="Bohuslän">Bohuslän</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Val_Camonica" title="Val Camonica">Val Camonica</a> in northern <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>.
</p><p>In the Mediterranean, the <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan civilization</a> was highly developed, with palace complexes from which sections of <a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">frescos</a> have been excavated. Contemporary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_art" title="Ancient Egyptian art">Ancient Egyptian art</a> and that of other advanced Near Eastern cultures can no longer be treated as "prehistoric".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iron_Age">Iron Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Iron Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hochdorf_golden_shoes_ornaments.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1960" data-file-width="2363" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Hochdorf_golden_shoes_ornaments.jpg/220px-Hochdorf_golden_shoes_ornaments.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Hochdorf_golden_shoes_ornaments.jpg/330px-Hochdorf_golden_shoes_ornaments.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Hochdorf_golden_shoes_ornaments.jpg/440px-Hochdorf_golden_shoes_ornaments.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hochdorf_golden_shoes_ornaments.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gold shoe plaques from the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> <a href="/wiki/Hochdorf_Chieftain%27s_Grave" title="Hochdorf Chieftain's Grave">Hochdorf Chieftain's Grave</a>, Germany, <span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 530</span> BC.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Celtic_art" title="Celtic art">Celtic art</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> saw the development of anthropomorphic sculptures, such as the <a href="/wiki/Warrior_of_Hirschlanden" title="Warrior of Hirschlanden">warrior of Hirschlanden</a>, and the statue from the <a href="/wiki/Glauberg" title="Glauberg">Glauberg</a>, Germany. <a href="/wiki/Hallstatt_culture" title="Hallstatt culture">Hallstatt</a> artists in the early Iron Age favored geometric, abstract designs perhaps influenced by trade links with the Classical world.
</p><p>The more elaborate and curvilinear <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La_T%C3%A8ne_style" title="La Tène style">La Tène style</a> developed in Europe in the later Iron Age from a centre in the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> valley but it soon spread across the continent. The rich chieftain classes appear to have encouraged ostentation and Classical influences such as bronze drinking vessels attest to a new fashion for wine drinking. Communal eating and drinking were an important part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celt" title="Celt">Celtic</a> society and culture and much of their art was often expressed through plates, knives, <a href="/wiki/Cauldron" title="Cauldron">cauldrons</a> and cups. <a href="/wiki/Horse_tack" title="Horse tack">Horse tack</a> and weaponry were also decorated. Mythical animals were a common motif along with religious and natural subjects and their depiction is a mix between the naturalistic and the stylized. <a href="/wiki/Megalithic_art" title="Megalithic art">Megalithic art</a> was still sometimes practiced, examples include the carved limestone pillars of the sanctuary at Entremont in modern-day France. Personal adornment included <a href="/wiki/Torc" title="Torc">torc</a> necklaces whilst the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">coinage</a> provided a further opportunity for artistic expression. The coins of this period are derivatives of Greek and Roman types, but showing the more exuberant Celtic artistic style.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Romano-Celtic_mirror_(Desborough).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="752" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Romano-Celtic_mirror_%28Desborough%29.jpg/220px-Romano-Celtic_mirror_%28Desborough%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Romano-Celtic_mirror_%28Desborough%29.jpg/330px-Romano-Celtic_mirror_%28Desborough%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Romano-Celtic_mirror_%28Desborough%29.jpg/440px-Romano-Celtic_mirror_%28Desborough%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Romano-Celtic_mirror_(Desborough).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1st century BCE mirror found in <a href="/wiki/Desborough" title="Desborough">Desborough</a>, England, showing the spiral and trumpet motif</div></div></div>
<p>The famous late 4th century BCE <a href="/wiki/Waldalgesheim_chariot_burial" title="Waldalgesheim chariot burial">Waldalgesheim chariot burial</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Rhineland" title="Rhineland">Rhineland</a> produced many fine examples of La Tène art including a bronze flagon and bronze plaques with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Repouss%C3%A9" title="Repoussé">repoussé</a> human figures. Many pieces had curvy, organic styles though to be derived from Classical tendril patterns.
</p><p>In much of western Europe elements of this artistic style can be discerned surviving in the art and architecture of the Roman colonies. In particular in Britain and Ireland there is a tenuous continuity through the Roman period, enabling Celtic motifs to resurface with new vigour in the Christian <a href="/wiki/Insular_art" title="Insular art">Insular art</a> from the 6th century onwards.
</p><p>The sophisticated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etruscan_culture" title="Etruscan culture">Etruscan culture</a> developed from the 9th to 2nd centuries, with considerable influence from the Greeks, before finally being absorbed by the Romans. By the end of the period they had developed writing, but early <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_art" title="Etruscan art">Etruscan art</a> can be called prehistoric.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a> falls outside the scope of this article; it had a close relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> in particular, known in this period as <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a>, where there were advanced cultures from the 4th millennium BCE, such as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A-Group" title="A-Group">A-Group</a>", "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C-Group" title="C-Group">C-Group</a>", and the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">Kingdom of Kush</a>.
</p>
