<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southern_Africa">Southern Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Southern Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cave_painting#Southern_Africa" title="Cave painting">Cave painting § Southern Africa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:San_Painting,_Ukalamba_Drakensberge_1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1494" data-file-width="2986" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/San_Painting%2C_Ukalamba_Drakensberge_1.JPG/220px-San_Painting%2C_Ukalamba_Drakensberge_1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/San_Painting%2C_Ukalamba_Drakensberge_1.JPG/330px-San_Painting%2C_Ukalamba_Drakensberge_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/San_Painting%2C_Ukalamba_Drakensberge_1.JPG/440px-San_Painting%2C_Ukalamba_Drakensberge_1.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:San_Painting,_Ukalamba_Drakensberge_1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/San_rock_art" title="San rock art">San rock painting</a> of an <a href="/wiki/Taurotragus" title="Taurotragus">eland</a> at <a href="/wiki/Drakensberg" title="Drakensberg">Drakensberg</a></div></div></div>
<p>In September 2018, scientists from the University of Bergen, the University of Bordeaux and the University of the Witwatersrand together reported the discovery of the earliest known drawing by <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> at <a href="/wiki/Blombos_Cave" title="Blombos Cave">Blombos Cave</a>, South Africa which is estimated to be 73,000 years old, much earlier than the 43,000 years old artifacts understood to be the earliest known modern human drawings found previously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20180912_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20180912-3">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is a significant body of rock painting in the region around <a href="/wiki/Matobo_National_Park" title="Matobo National Park">Matobo National Park</a> of Zimbabwe dating from as early as 6000 BCE to 500 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoulsonCampbell200186_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoulsonCampbell200186-48">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Significant <a href="/wiki/San_rock_art" title="San rock art">San rock paintings</a> exist in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waterberg_National_Park" title="Waterberg National Park">Waterberg</a> area above the <a href="/wiki/Palala_River" title="Palala River">Palala River</a> and around <a href="/wiki/Drakensberg" title="Drakensberg">Drakensberg</a> in South Africa, some of which are considered to derive from the period 8000 BCE. These images are very clear and depict a variety of human and <a href="/wiki/Wildlife" title="Wildlife">wildlife</a> motifs, especially <a href="/wiki/Antelope" title="Antelope">antelope</a>. There appears to be a fairly continuous history of rock painting in this area; some of the art clearly dates into the 19th century. They include depictions of horses with riders, which were not introduced to the area until the 1820s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoulsonCampbell200180–82_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoulsonCampbell200180–82-49">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Namibia, in addition to the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11_Cave" title="Apollo 11 Cave">Apollo 11 Cave</a> complex, has a significant array of San rock art near <a href="/wiki/Twyfelfontein" title="Twyfelfontein">Twyfelfontein</a>. This work is several thousand years old, and appears to end with the arrival of pastoral tribes in the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Horn_of_Africa">Horn of Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Horn of Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cave_painting#Horn_of_Africa" title="Cave painting">Cave painting § Horn of Africa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Laas_Geel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="333" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Laas_Geel.jpg/220px-Laas_Geel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Laas_Geel.jpg/330px-Laas_Geel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Laas_Geel.jpg/440px-Laas_Geel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Laas_Geel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Long-horned <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Rock_art" title="Rock art">rock art</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Laas_Geel" title="Laas Geel">Laas Geel</a> complex.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Laas_Geel" title="Laas Geel">Laas Geel</a> is a complex of <a href="/wiki/Cave" title="Cave">caves</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rock_shelter" title="Rock shelter">rock shelters</a> in northwestern <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>. Famous for their rock art, the caves are located in a rural area on the outskirts of <a href="/wiki/Hargeisa" title="Hargeisa">Hargeisa</a>. They contain some of the earliest known cave paintings in the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>, many of which depict pastoral scenes. Laas Geel's rock art is estimated to date back to somewhere between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/9th_millennium_BCE" title="9th millennium BCE">9,000</a>–<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/8th_millennium_BCE" title="8th millennium BCE">8,000</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3rd_millennium_BCE" title="3rd millennium BCE">3,000</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BCE" title="BCE">BCE</a>.
</p><p>In 2008, archaeologists also announced the discovery of cave paintings in Somalia's northern <a href="/wiki/Dhambalin" title="Dhambalin">Dhambalin</a> region, which the researchers suggest includes one of the earliest known depictions of a hunter on horseback. The rock art is in the Ethiopian-Arabian style, dated to 1000 to 3000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tdodras_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tdodras-51">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guafcpaonas_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guafcpaonas-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other prehistoric art in the Horn region include stone <a href="/wiki/Megalith" title="Megalith">megaliths</a> and engravings, some of which are 3,500 years old. The town of Dillo in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> has a hilltop covered with stone <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stelae</a>. It is one of several such sites in southern Ethiopia dating from historic period<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> (10th–14th centuries).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoulsonCampbell2001147_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoulsonCampbell2001147-53">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Saharan_Africa">Saharan Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Saharan Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Saharan_rock_art" title="Saharan rock art">Saharan rock art</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rock_art#Sahara" title="Rock art">Rock art § Sahara</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sleeping_Antelope_Tin_Taghirt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2722" data-file-width="3852" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Sleeping_Antelope_Tin_Taghirt.jpg/220px-Sleeping_Antelope_Tin_Taghirt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Sleeping_Antelope_Tin_Taghirt.jpg/330px-Sleeping_Antelope_Tin_Taghirt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Sleeping_Antelope_Tin_Taghirt.jpg/440px-Sleeping_Antelope_Tin_Taghirt.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sleeping_Antelope_Tin_Taghirt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Petroglyph" title="Petroglyph">petroglyphic</a> <a href="/wiki/Saharan_rock_art" title="Saharan rock art">Saharan rock carving</a> from southern <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a> depicting an <a href="/wiki/Antelope" title="Antelope">antelope</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gazelle" title="Gazelle">gazelle</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The early art of this region has been divided into five periods:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Bubalus_Period" title="Bubalus Period">Bubalus Period</a>, roughly 12-8 kya</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Round_Head_Period" title="Round Head Period">Round Head Period</a>, roughly 10-8 kya</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pastoral_period" title="Pastoral period">Pastoral Period</a>, roughly 7.5-4 kya</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Horse_Period&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Horse Period (page does not exist)">Horse Period</a>, roughly 3-2 kya</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Camel_Period&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Camel Period (page does not exist)">Camel Period</a>, 2,000 years ago to the present</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Libya_5041_Petroglyphs_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Libya_5041_Petroglyphs_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg/220px-Libya_5041_Petroglyphs_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Libya_5041_Petroglyphs_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg/330px-Libya_5041_Petroglyphs_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Libya_5041_Petroglyphs_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg/440px-Libya_5041_Petroglyphs_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Libya_5041_Petroglyphs_Tadrart_Acacus_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rock carving of an elephant at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tadrart_Acacus" title="Tadrart Acacus">Tadrart Acacus</a></div></div></div>
<p>Works of the Bubalus period span the Sahara, with the finest work, carvings of naturalistically depicted megafauna, concentrated in the central highlands. The Round Head Period is dominated by paintings of strangely shaped human forms, and few animals, suggesting the artists were foragers.These artists may have been producing rock art 2,000 years before domestication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoukopova2012CH.4_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoukopova2012CH.4-54">[53]</a></sup> These works are largely limited to <a href="/wiki/Tassili_n%27Ajjer" title="Tassili n'Ajjer">Tassili n'Ajjer</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tadrart_Acacus" title="Tadrart Acacus">Tadrart Acacus</a>. Some works show figures with bows near herds of cattle and a reunion of members near cattle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-55">[54]</a></sup>Round Heads are mostly found in Tassili and similar isolated Round-Head figures have been found in areas such as Jebel Uweinat and Ennedi. Which may indicate similar cultures between these populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoukopova2012CH.4_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoukopova2012CH.4-54">[53]</a></sup>
Other animals represented in the Round head art are hippopotamus, elephant, and bovids. Animals like antelope and mouflon are also represented; fauna remains have been found in archaeological sites during excavations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoukopova2012CH.4_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoukopova2012CH.4-54">[53]</a></sup> About 90% of Round Head art is animal representation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoukopova2012CH.4_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoukopova2012CH.4-54">[53]</a></sup> Toward the end of the period, images of domesticated animals, as well as decorative clothing and headdresses appear. Some domesticated animals are cattle from the <i>Bovidae</i> family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoukopova2012CH.4_54-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoukopova2012CH.4-54">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pastoral Period art was more focused on domestic scenes, including herding and dancing. As well as hunting scenes of mouflon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoukopova2012CH.4_54-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoukopova2012CH.4-54">[53]</a></sup> Unlike in the Round Head art, the bow is relevant to Pastoral art since hunting scenes were depicted during this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoukopova2012CH.3_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoukopova2012CH.3-56">[55]</a></sup> Pastoral figures are seen on the same wall of Round Heads except they are along the borders. The foragers may have been respecting the previous artwork and did not paint over the figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoukopova2012CH.4_54-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoukopova2012CH.4-54">[53]</a></sup> The quality of artwork declined, as figures became more simplified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoulsonCampbell2001156–160_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoulsonCampbell2001156–160-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Horse Period began in the eastern Sahara and spread west. Depictions from this period include carvings and paintings of horses, chariots, and warriors with metal weapons, although there are also frequent depictions of wildlife such as giraffes. Humans are generally depicted in a stylized way. Some of the chariot art bears resemblance to temple carvings from ancient <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. Occasionally, art panels are accompanied by <a href="/wiki/Tifinagh" title="Tifinagh">Tifinagh</a> script, still in use by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">Berber people</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_people" title="Tuareg people">Tuareg</a> today; however, modern Tuareg are generally unable to read these inscriptions. The final Camel period features carvings and paintings in which camels predominate, but also include humans with swords, and later, guns; the art of this time is relatively crude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoulsonCampbell2001160–162,_205_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoulsonCampbell2001160–162,_205-58">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_Africa">North Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: North Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cave_painting#North_Africa" title="Cave painting">Cave painting § North Africa</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Americas">The Americas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: The Americas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_art" title="Pre-Columbian art">Pre-Columbian art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Visual_arts_by_indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Visual arts by indigenous peoples of the Americas">Visual arts by indigenous peoples of the Americas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_pre-Columbian_cultures" title="List of pre-Columbian cultures">List of pre-Columbian cultures</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Serpent_Mound_(aerial_view).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1355" data-file-width="909" decoding="async" height="328" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Serpent_Mound_%28aerial_view%29.jpg/220px-Serpent_Mound_%28aerial_view%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Serpent_Mound_%28aerial_view%29.jpg/330px-Serpent_Mound_%28aerial_view%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Serpent_Mound_%28aerial_view%29.jpg/440px-Serpent_Mound_%28aerial_view%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Serpent_Mound_(aerial_view).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Serpent_Mound" title="Great Serpent Mound">Great Serpent Mound</a>, a 411-meter long (1,348 ft) <a href="/wiki/Effigy_mound" title="Effigy mound">effigy mound</a> in <a href="/wiki/Adams_County,_Ohio" title="Adams County, Ohio">Adams County, Ohio</a>, ca. 1070 CE</div></div></div>
<p>Belonging in the Lithic stage, the oldest known art in the Americas is the <a href="/wiki/Vero_man" title="Vero man">Vero Beach</a> bone, possibly a mammoth bone, etched with a profile of walking mammoth that dates back to 11,000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> The oldest known painted object in the Americas is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cooper_Bison_Skull" title="Cooper Bison Skull">Cooper Bison Skull</a> from 10,900 to 10,200 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bement_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-bement-60">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Mesoamerica">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Maya_art" title="Ancient Maya art">Ancient Maya art</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olmec#art" title="Olmec">Olmec § art</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Visual_arts_by_indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas#Mesoamerica_and_Central_America" title="Visual arts by indigenous peoples of the Americas">Visual arts by indigenous peoples of the Americas § Mesoamerica and Central America</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CabezaColosal1_MuseoXalapa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1800" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/CabezaColosal1_MuseoXalapa.jpg/220px-CabezaColosal1_MuseoXalapa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/CabezaColosal1_MuseoXalapa.jpg/330px-CabezaColosal1_MuseoXalapa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/CabezaColosal1_MuseoXalapa.jpg/440px-CabezaColosal1_MuseoXalapa.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CabezaColosal1_MuseoXalapa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olmec" title="Olmec">Olmec</a> stone head</div></div></div>
<p>The ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olmec" title="Olmec">Olmec</a> "Bird Vessel" and bowl, both <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">ceramic</a> and dating to circa 1000 BC as well as other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceramics_(art)" title="Ceramics (art)">ceramics</a> were produced in <a href="/wiki/Kiln" title="Kiln">kilns</a> capable of exceeding approximately 900 °C. The only other <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistoric</a> culture known to have achieved such high temperatures is that of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Friedman_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Friedman-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Much Olmec art is highly stylized and uses an iconography reflective of the religious meaning of the artworks. Some Olmec art, however, is surprisingly naturalistic, displaying an accuracy of depiction of human anatomy perhaps equaled in the pre-Columbian New World only by the best Maya Classic-era art. Olmec art-forms emphasize monumental statuary and small <a href="/wiki/Jade" title="Jade">jade</a> carvings. A common theme is to be found in representations of a divine <a href="/wiki/Jaguar" title="Jaguar">jaguar</a>. <a href="/wiki/Olmec_figurine" title="Olmec figurine">Olmec figurines</a> were also found abundantly through their period.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_America">South America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: South America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lithic age art in South America includes Monte Alegre culture rock paintings created at <a href="/wiki/Caverna_da_Pedra_Pintada" title="Caverna da Pedra Pintada">Caverna da Pedra Pintada</a> dating back to 9250–8550 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guitarrero_Cave" title="Guitarrero Cave">Guitarrero Cave</a> in Peru has the earliest known textiles in South America, dating to 8000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199517_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199517-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Peru_and_the_central_Andes">Peru and the central Andes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Peru and the central Andes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultural_periods_of_Peru" title="Cultural periods of Peru">Cultural periods of Peru</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Peru" title="History of Peru">History of Peru</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Andean_textiles" title="Andean textiles">Andean textiles</a></div>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Lithic_and_preceramic_periods">Lithic and preceramic periods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Lithic and preceramic periods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CupisniqueLMC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="816" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/CupisniqueLMC.jpg/220px-CupisniqueLMC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/CupisniqueLMC.jpg/330px-CupisniqueLMC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/CupisniqueLMC.jpg/440px-CupisniqueLMC.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CupisniqueLMC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Stirrup_spout_vessel" title="Stirrup spout vessel">Stirrup handled</a> Cupinisque ceramic vase 1250 BCE from the <a href="/wiki/Larco_Museum" title="Larco Museum">Larco Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, including an area of the central <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a> stretching from the northern part of the country to northern <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, has a rich cultural history, with evidence of human habitation dating to roughly 10,000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELavallée199588_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELavallée199588-65">[64]</a></sup> Prior to the emergence of ceramics in this region around 1850 BCE, cave paintings and beads have been found. These finds include rock paintings that controversially date as far back as 9500 BCE in the <a href="/wiki/Toquepala_Caves" title="Toquepala Caves">Toquepala Caves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELavallée199594_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELavallée199594-66">[65]</a></sup> Burial sites in Peru like one at Telarmachay as old as 8600–7200 BCE contained evidence of ritual burial, with red ocher and bead necklaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELavallée1995115_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELavallée1995115-67">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest ceramics that appear in Peru may have been imported from the Validivia region; indigenous pottery production almost certainly arrived in the highlands around 1800 BCE at <a href="/wiki/Kotosh" title="Kotosh">Kotosh</a>, and on the coast at La Florida c. 1700 BCE. Older calabash gourd vessels with human faces burned into them were found at <a href="/wiki/Huaca_Prieta" title="Huaca Prieta">Huaca Prieta</a>, a site dating to 2500–2000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELavallée1995186_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELavallée1995186-68">[67]</a></sup> Huaca Prieta also contained some early patterned and dyed textiles made from twisted plant fibers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruhns199480_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruhns199480-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_Period_and_First_Horizon">Initial Period and First Horizon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Initial Period and First Horizon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Chav%C3%ADn_culture" title="Chavín culture">Chavín culture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Estela.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="267" data-file-width="402" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Estela.jpg/220px-Estela.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Estela.jpg/330px-Estela.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/43/Estela.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Estela.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An image of the <a href="/wiki/Lanz%C3%B3n" title="Lanzón">Lanzón</a> deity on the great wall at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chav%C3%ADn_de_Huantar" title="Chavín de Huantar">Chavín de Huantar</a>, a First Horizon site</div></div></div>
<p>The Initial Period in Central Andean cultures lasted roughly from 1800 BCE to 900 BCE. Textiles from this time found at Huaca Prieta are of astonishing complexity, including images such as crabs whose claws transform into snakes, and double-headed birds. Many of these images are similar to <a href="/wiki/Optical_illusion" title="Optical illusion">optical illusions</a>, where which image dominates depends in part on which the viewer chooses to see. Other portable artwork from this time includes decorated mirrors, bone and shell jewelry, and unfired clay female effigies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199519–20_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199519–20-70">[69]</a></sup> Public architecture, including works estimated to require the movement of more than 100,000 tons of stone, are to be found at sites like Kotosh, <a href="/wiki/El_Para%C3%ADso,_Peru" title="El Paraíso, Peru">El Paraíso, Peru</a>, and <a href="/wiki/La_Galgada_(archaeological_site)" title="La Galgada (archaeological site)">La Galgada (archaeological site)</a>. Kotosh, a site in the Andean highlands, is especially noted as the site of the Temple of the Crossed Hands, in which there are two reliefs of crossed forearms, one pair male, one pair female.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199521_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199521-71">[70]</a></sup> Also of note is one of South America's largest ceremonial sites, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sech%C3%ADn_Alto" title="Sechín Alto">Sechín Alto</a>. This site's crowning work is a twelve-story platform, with stones incised with military themes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199527_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199527-72">[71]</a></sup> The architecture and art of the highlands, in particular, laid down the groundwork for the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Chav%C3%ADn_culture" title="Chavín culture">Chavín culture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199522_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199522-73">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Chavín culture dominated the central Andes during the First Horizon, beginning around 900 BCE, and is generally divided into two stages. The first, running until about 500 BCE, represented a significant cultural unification of the highland and coastal cultures of the time. Imagery in all manner of art (textiles, ceramics, jewelry, and architectural) included sometimes fantastic imagery such as jaguars, snakes, and human–animal composites, much of it seemingly inspired by the jungles to the east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199528–29_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199528–29-74">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>The later stage of the Chavín culture is primarily represented by a significant architectural expansion of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chav%C3%ADn_de_Huantar" title="Chavín de Huantar">Chavín de Huantar</a> site around 500 BCE, accompanied by a set of stylistic changes. This expansion included, among other changes, over forty large stone heads, whose reconstructed positions represent a transformation from human to supernatural animal visages. Much of the other art at the complex from this time contains such supernatural imagery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199540_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199540-75">[74]</a></sup> The portable art associated with this time included sophisticated metalworking, including alloying of metals and soldering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199544_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199544-76">[75]</a></sup> Textiles found at sites like <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Karwa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Karwa (page does not exist)">Karwa</a> clearly depict Chavín cultural influences,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199546_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199546-77">[76]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Cupisnique" title="Cupisnique">Cupisnique</a> style of pottery disseminated by the Chavín would set standards all across the region for later cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199549_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199549-78">[77]</a></sup> (The vessel pictured at the top of this article, while from the later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moche_(culture)" title="Moche (culture)">Moche</a> culture, is representative of the stirrup-spouted vessels of the Chavín.)
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Intermediate_Period">Early Intermediate Period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Early Intermediate Period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Paracas_culture" title="Paracas culture">Paracas culture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nazca_culture" title="Nazca culture">Nazca culture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Moche_culture" title="Moche culture">Moche culture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paracas_Mantle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="330" data-file-width="438" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Paracas_Mantle.jpg/220px-Paracas_Mantle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Paracas_Mantle.jpg/330px-Paracas_Mantle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/93/Paracas_Mantle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paracas_Mantle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Paracas Mantle dating from 200 CE</div></div></div>
<p>The Early Intermediate Period lasted from about 200 BCE to 600 CE. Late in the First Horizon, the Chavín culture began to decline, and other cultures, predominantly in the coastal areas, began to develop. The earliest of these was the <a href="/wiki/Paracas_culture" title="Paracas culture">Paracas culture</a>, centered on the <a href="/wiki/Paracas_Peninsula" title="Paracas Peninsula">Paracas Peninsula</a> of central Peru. Active from 600 BCE to 175 BCE, their early work clearly shows Chavín influence, but a locally distinctive style and technique developed. It was characterized by technical and time-consuming detail work, visually colorful, and a profusion visual elements. Distinctive technical differences include painting on clay after firing, and embroidery on textiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199550_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199550-79">[78]</a></sup> One notable find is a mantle that was clearly used for training purposes; it shows obvious indications of experts doing some of the weaving, interspersed with less technically proficient trainee work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199558_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199558-80">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nazca-lineas-perro-c01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1616" data-file-width="1877" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Nazca-lineas-perro-c01.jpg/220px-Nazca-lineas-perro-c01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Nazca-lineas-perro-c01.jpg/330px-Nazca-lineas-perro-c01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Nazca-lineas-perro-c01.jpg/440px-Nazca-lineas-perro-c01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nazca-lineas-perro-c01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazca_lines" title="Nazca lines">Nazca line</a> figure known as The Dog</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nazca_culture" title="Nazca culture">Nazca culture</a> of southern Peru, which is widely known for the enormous figures traced on the ground by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazca_lines" title="Nazca lines">Nazca lines</a> in southern Peru, shared some similarities with the Paracas culture, but techniques (and scale) differed. The Nazca painted their ceramics with <a href="/wiki/Slip_(ceramics)" title="Slip (ceramics)">slip</a>, and also painted their textiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199567_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199567-81">[80]</a></sup> Nazca ceramics featured a wide variety of subjects, from the mundane to the fantastic, including utilitarian vessels and effigy figures. The Nazca also excelled at goldsmithing, and made <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pan_pipes" title="Pan pipes">pan pipes</a> from clay in a style not unlike the pipes heard in music of the Andes today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199574–75_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199574–75-82">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>The famous Nazca lines are accompanied by temple-like constructions (showing no sign of permanent habitation) and open plazas that presumably had ritual purposes related to the lines. The lines themselves are laid out on a sort of natural blackboard, where a thin layer of dark stone covers lighter stone; the lines were thus created by simply removing the top layer where desired, after using surveying techniques to lay out the design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199578–82_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199578–82-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mochica_headdress_Larco_museum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1132" data-file-width="1178" decoding="async" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Mochica_headdress_Larco_museum.jpg/220px-Mochica_headdress_Larco_museum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Mochica_headdress_Larco_museum.jpg/330px-Mochica_headdress_Larco_museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Mochica_headdress_Larco_museum.jpg/440px-Mochica_headdress_Larco_museum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mochica_headdress_Larco_museum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gold <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moche_(culture)" title="Moche (culture)">Moche</a> headdress representing a <a href="/wiki/Andean_condor" title="Andean condor">condor</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the north of Peru, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moche_(culture)" title="Moche (culture)">Moche</a> culture dominated during this time. Also known as <i>Mochica</i> or <i>Early Chimú</i>, this warlike culture dominated the area until about 500 CE, apparently using conquest to gain access to critical resources along the desert coast: arable land and water. Moche art is again notably distinctive, expressive and dynamic in a way that many other Andean cultures were not. Knowledge of the period has been notably expanded by finds like the pristine royal tombs at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sip%C3%A1n" title="Sipán">Sipán</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199583_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199583-84">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Moche very obviously absorbed some elements of the Chavín culture, but also absorbed ideas from smaller nearby cultures that they assimilated, such as the <a href="/wiki/Recuay_culture" title="Recuay culture">Recuay culture</a> and the Vicús.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199588_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199588-85">[84]</a></sup> They made fully sculpted ceramic animal figures, worked gold, and wove textiles. The art often featured everyday images, but seemingly always with a ritual intent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199586_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199586-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>In its later years, the Moche came under the influence of the expanding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huari_culture" title="Huari culture">Huari</a> empire. The Cerro Blanco site of <a href="/wiki/Huaca_del_Sol" title="Huaca del Sol">Huaca del Sol</a> appears to have been the Moche capital. Largely destroyed by natural events around 600 CE, it was further damaged by Spanish conquistadors searching for gold, and continues with modern looters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199592_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller199592-87">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Horizon">Middle Horizon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Middle Horizon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tiwanaku1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1667" data-file-width="1160" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Tiwanaku1.jpg/160px-Tiwanaku1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Tiwanaku1.jpg/240px-Tiwanaku1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Tiwanaku1.jpg/320px-Tiwanaku1.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tiwanaku1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ponce monolith in the sunken courtyard of the Tiwanaku's Kalasasaya temple</div></div></div>
<p>The Middle Horizon lasted from 600 CE to 1000 CE, and was dominated by two cultures: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huari_culture" title="Huari culture">Huari</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tiwanaku" title="Tiwanaku">Tiwanaku</a>. The Tiwanaku (also spelled <i>Tiahuanaco</i>) culture arose near <a href="/wiki/Lake_Titicaca" title="Lake Titicaca">Lake Titicaca</a> (on the modern border between Peru and <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>), while the Wari culture arose in the southern highlands of Peru. Both cultures appear to have been influenced by the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Pukara_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Pukara culture (page does not exist)">Pukara culture</a>, which was active during the Early Intermediate in between the primary centers of the Wari and Tiwanaku.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995121–123_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995121–123-88">[87]</a></sup> These cultures both had wide-ranging influence, and shared some common features in their portable art, but their monumental arts were somewhat distinctive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995119_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995119-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>The monumental art of the Tiwanaku demonstrated technical prowess in stonework, including fine detailed reliefs, and monoliths such as the Ponce monolith (photo to the left), and the Sun Gate, both in the main Tiwanaku site. The portable art featured "portrait vessels", with figured heads on ceramic vessels, as well as natural imagery like jaguars and raptors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995131–134_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995131–134-90">[89]</a></sup> A full range of materials, from ceramics to textiles to wood, bone, and shell, were used in creative endeavours. Textiles with a weave of 300 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thread_count" title="Thread count">threads per inch</a> (80 threads per cm) have been found at Tiwanaku sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995136_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995136-91">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Piquillacta_view4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1176" data-file-width="1723" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Piquillacta_view4.jpg/220px-Piquillacta_view4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Piquillacta_view4.jpg/330px-Piquillacta_view4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Piquillacta_view4.jpg/440px-Piquillacta_view4.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Piquillacta_view4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The ruins of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pikillacta" title="Pikillacta">Pikillacta</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huari_culture" title="Huari culture">Wari</a> site</div></div></div>
<p>The Wari dominated an area from northern to central Peru, with their main center near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ayacucho,_Peru" title="Ayacucho, Peru">Ayacucho</a>. Their art is distinguished from the Tiwanaku style by the use of bolder colors and patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995138–139_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995138–139-92">[91]</a></sup> Notable among Wari finds are tapestry garments, presumed to be made for priests or rulers to wear, often bearing abstract geometric designs of significant complexity, but also bearing images of animals and figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995146–148_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995146–148-93">[92]</a></sup> Wari ceramics, also of high technical quality, are similar in many ways to those of the preceding cultures, where local influences from fallen cultures, like the Moche, are still somewhat evident. Metalwork, while rarely found due to its desirability by looters, shows elegant simplicity and, once more, a high level of workmanship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995149–150_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995149–150-94">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Intermediate_Period">Late Intermediate Period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Late Intermediate Period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Following the decline of the Wari and Tiwanaku, the northern and central coastal areas were somewhat dominated by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chim%C3%BA_culture" title="Chimú culture">Chimú culture</a>, which included notable subcultures like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sican_Culture" title="Sican Culture">Lambayeque (or Sicán)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chancay_culture" title="Chancay culture">Chancay</a> cultures. To the south, coastal cultures dominated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ica_region" title="Ica region">Ica region</a>, and there was a significant cultural crossroads at <a href="/wiki/Pachacamac" title="Pachacamac">Pachacamac</a>, near Lima.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995151_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995151-95">[94]</a></sup> These cultures would dominate from about 1000 CE until the 1460s and 1470s, as the <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca Empire</a> began to take shape and eventually absorbed the geographically smaller nearby cultures.
</p><p><b>Chimú and Sicán Cultures</b>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sican_funerary_mask_in_the_Metropolitan_Museum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Sican_funerary_mask_in_the_Metropolitan_Museum.jpg/290px-Sican_funerary_mask_in_the_Metropolitan_Museum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Sican_funerary_mask_in_the_Metropolitan_Museum.jpg/435px-Sican_funerary_mask_in_the_Metropolitan_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Sican_funerary_mask_in_the_Metropolitan_Museum.jpg/580px-Sican_funerary_mask_in_the_Metropolitan_Museum.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sican_funerary_mask_in_the_Metropolitan_Museum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sic%C3%A1n" title="Sicán">Sicán</a> funerary mask, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Museum" title="Metropolitan Museum">Metropolitan Museum</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chim%C3%BA_culture" title="Chimú culture">Chimú culture</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sican_Culture" title="Sican Culture">Sican Culture</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chim%C3%BA_culture" title="Chimú culture">Chimú culture</a> in particular was responsible for an extremely large number of artworks. Its capital city, <a href="/wiki/Chan_Chan" title="Chan Chan">Chan Chan</a>, appears to have contained building that appeared to function as museums—they seem to have been used for displaying and preserving artwork. Much of the artwork from Chan Chan in particular has been looted, some by the Spanish after the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_the_Inca_Empire" title="Spanish conquest of the Inca Empire">Spanish conquest</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995151_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995151-95">[94]</a></sup> The art from this time at times displays amazing complexity, with "multimedia" works that require artists working together in a diversity of media, including materials believed to have come from as far away as Central America. Items of increasing splendor or value were produced, apparently as the society became increasing stratified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995153_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995153-96">[95]</a></sup> At the same, the quality of some of the work declined, as demand for pieces pushed production rates up and values down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995154_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995154-97">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sicán culture flourished from 700 CE to about 1400 CE, although it came under political domination of the Chimú around 1100 CE, at which time many of its artists may have moved to Chan Chan. There was significant copperworking by the Sicán, including what seems to be a sort of currency based on copper objects that look like axes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995156_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995156-98">[97]</a></sup> Artwork includes burial masks, beakers and metal vessels that previous cultures traditionally made of clay. The metalwork of the Sicán was particularly sophisticated, with innovations including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Repouss%C3%A9" title="Repoussé">repoussé</a> and shell inlay. Sheet metal was also often used to cover other works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995156–158_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995156–158-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Prominent in Sicán iconography is the Sicán deity, which appears on all manner of work, from the portable to the monumental. Other imagery includes geometric and wave patterns, as well as scenes of fishing and shell diving.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995160_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995160-100">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Chancay culture</b>
<a href="/wiki/Chancay_culture" title="Chancay culture">Chancay culture</a>, before it was subsumed by the Chimú, did not feature notable monumental art. Ceramics and textiles were made, but the quality and skill level was uneven. Ceramics are generally black on white, and often suffer from flaws like poor firing, and drips of the slip used for color; however, fine examples exist. Textiles are overall of a higher quality, including the use of painted weaves and tapestry techniques, and were produced in large quantities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995175–177_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995175–177-101">[100]</a></sup> The color palette of the Chancay was not overly bold: golds, browns, white, and scarlet predominate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995179_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995179-102">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Pachacamac</b>
<a href="/wiki/Pachacamac" title="Pachacamac">Pachacamac</a> is a temple site south of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lima,_Peru" title="Lima, Peru">Lima, Peru</a> that was an important pilgrimage center into Spanish colonial times. The site boasts temple constructions from several periods, culminating in Inca constructions that are still in relatively good condition. The temples were painted with murals depicting plants and animals. The main temple contained a carved wooden sculpture akin to a <a href="/wiki/Totem_pole" title="Totem pole">totem pole</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995179_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995179-102">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Ica culture</b>
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ica_region" title="Ica region">Ica region</a>, which had been dominated by the Nazca, was fragmented into several smaller political and culture groups. The pottery produced in this region was of the highest quality at the time, and its aesthetics would be adopted by the Inca when they conquered the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995180_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995180-103">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Horizon_and_Inca_culture">Late Horizon and Inca culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Late Horizon and Inca culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Inca_architecture" title="Inca architecture">Inca architecture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CuscoPiedra12angulo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1082" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/CuscoPiedra12angulo.jpg/220px-CuscoPiedra12angulo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/CuscoPiedra12angulo.jpg/330px-CuscoPiedra12angulo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/CuscoPiedra12angulo.jpg/440px-CuscoPiedra12angulo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CuscoPiedra12angulo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i>twelve angle stone</i>, in the Hatum Rumiyoc street of <a href="/wiki/Cusco" title="Cusco">Cusco</a>, is an example of Inca masonry.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tupa-inca-tunic.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="944" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Tupa-inca-tunic.png/220px-Tupa-inca-tunic.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Tupa-inca-tunic.png/330px-Tupa-inca-tunic.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Tupa-inca-tunic.png/440px-Tupa-inca-tunic.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tupa-inca-tunic.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Inca period tunic</div></div></div>
<p>This time period represents the era in which the culture of the central Andes is almost completely dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca Empire</a>, which began its expansion in 1438. It lasted until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_Peru" title="Spanish conquest of Peru">Spanish conquest</a> in 1533. The Inca absorbed much technical skill from the cultures they conquered, and disseminated it, along with standard shapes and patterns, throughout their area of influence, which extended from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quito,_Ecuador" title="Quito, Ecuador">Quito, Ecuador</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Santiago,_Chile" title="Santiago, Chile">Santiago, Chile</a>. Inca stonework is notably proficient; giant stones are set so tightly without mortar that a knife blade will not fit in the gap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995181_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995181-104">[103]</a></sup> Many of the Inca's monumental structures deliberately echoed the natural environment around them; this is particularly evident in some of the structures at <a href="/wiki/Machu_Picchu" title="Machu Picchu">Machu Picchu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995190_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995190-105">[104]</a></sup> The Inca laid the city of <a href="/wiki/Cusco" title="Cusco">Cusco</a> in the shape of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puma_(animal)" title="Puma (animal)">puma</a>, with the head of the puma at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacsayhuaman" title="Sacsayhuaman">Sacsayhuaman</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995194_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995194-106">[105]</a></sup> a shape that is still discernible in aerial photographs of the city today.
</p><p>The iconography of Inca art, while clearly drawing from its many predecessors, is still recognizably Inca. Bronzework owes a clear debt to the Chimú, as do a number of cultural traditions: the finest goods were reserved to the rulers, who wore the finest textiles, and ate and drank from gold and silver vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995186_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995186-107">[106]</a></sup> As a result, Inca metalwork was relatively rare, and an obvious source of plunder for the conquering Spanish.
</p><p>Textiles were widely prized within the empire, in part as they were somewhat more portable in the far-flung empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995209_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995209-108">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ceramics were made in large quantities, and, as with other media, in standardized shapes and patterns. One common shape is the <i>urpu</i>, a distinctive urn shape that came in a wide variety of standard capacities, much as modern storage containers do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995215_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995215-109">[108]</a></sup> In spite of this standardization, many local areas retained some distinctive aspects of their culture in the works they produced; ceramics produced in areas under significant Chimú control prior to the Inca rule still retain characteristics indicative of that style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995216_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995216-110">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the Spanish conquest, the art of the central Andes was significantly affected by the conflict and diseases brought by the Spanish. Early colonial period art, began to show influences of both Christianity and Inca religious and artistic ideas, and eventually also began to encompass new techniques brought by the conquerors, including oil painting on canvas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995217_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStone-Miller1995217-111">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_ceramics_in_northern_South_America">Early ceramics in northern South America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Early ceramics in northern South America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_in_Colombia" title="Indigenous peoples in Colombia">Indigenous peoples in Colombia</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_ceramics_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous ceramics of the Americas">Indigenous ceramics of the Americas</a></div>
<p>The earliest evidence of decorated pottery in South America is to be found in two places. A variety of sites in the Santarém region of <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> contain ceramic sherds dating to a period between 5000 and 3000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELavallée1995182_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELavallée1995182-112">[111]</a></sup> Sites in <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, at <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mons%C3%BA,_Colombia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Monsú, Colombia (page does not exist)">Monsú</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Jacinto,_Colombia" title="San Jacinto, Colombia">San Jacinto</a> contained pottery finds in different styles, and date as far back as 3500 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruhns1994116–117_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruhns1994116–117-113">[112]</a></sup> This is an area of active research and subject to change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELavallée1995176–182_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELavallée1995176–182-114">[113]</a></sup>  The ceramics were decorated with curvilinear incisions. Another ancient <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Hormiga_archaeological_site" title="Puerto Hormiga archaeological site">site at Puerto Hormiga</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Bol%C3%ADvar_Department" title="Bolívar Department">Bolívar Department</a> of <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> dating to 3100 BCE contained pottery fragments that included figured animals in a style related to later Barrancoid cultural finds in Colombia and <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruhns1994116–117_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruhns1994116–117-113">[112]</a></sup> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Valdivia,_Ecuador&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Valdivia, Ecuador (page does not exist)">Valdivia, Ecuador</a> also has a site dated to roughly 3100 BCE containing decorated fragments, as well as figurines, many represent nude females. The Valdivian style stretched as far south as northern Peru,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruhns1994117–118_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruhns1994117–118-115">[114]</a></sup> and may, according to Lavallée, yet yield older artifacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELavallée1995182_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELavallée1995182-112">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 2000 BCE, pottery was evident in eastern Venezuela. The La Gruta style, often painted in red or white, included incised animal figures in the ceramic, as well as ceramic vessels shaped as animal effigies. The Rancho Peludo style of western Venezuela featured relatively simple textile-type decorations and incisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruhns1994117–118_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruhns1994117–118-115">[114]</a></sup> Finds in the central Andes dating to 1800 BCE and later appear to be derived from the Valdivian tradition of Ecuador.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruhns1994119_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruhns1994119-116">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_art_in_eastern_South_America">Early art in eastern South America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Early art in eastern South America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_in_Brazil" title="Indigenous peoples in Brazil">Indigenous peoples in Brazil</a></div>
<p>Relatively little is known about the early settlement of much of South America east of the Andes. This is due to the lack of stone (generally required for leaving durable artifacts), and a <a href="/wiki/Jungle" title="Jungle">jungle</a> environment that rapidly recycles organic materials. Beyond the Andean regions, where the inhabitants were more clearly related to the early cultures of Peru, early finds are generally limited to coastal areas and those areas where there are stone outcrops. While there is evidence of human habitation in northern Brazil as early as 8000 BCE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELavallée1995113_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELavallée1995113-117">[116]</a></sup> and rock art of unknown (or at best uncertain) age, ceramics appear to be the earliest artistic artifacts. The Mina civilization of Brazil (3000–1600 BCE) had simple round vessels with a red wash, that were stylistic predecessors to later Bahia and Guyanese cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruhns1994117–118_115-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruhns1994117–118-115">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Southern_South_America">Southern South America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Southern South America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The southern reaches of South America show evidence of human habitation as far back as 10,000 BCE. A site at <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Arroio_do_Fosseis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Arroio do Fosseis (page does not exist)">Arroio do Fosseis</a> on the pampa in southern Brazil has shown reliable evidence to that time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELavallée1995108_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELavallée1995108-118">[117]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Tierra_del_Fuego" title="Tierra del Fuego">Tierra del Fuego</a> at the southern tip of the continent has been occupied since 7000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELavallée1995112_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELavallée1995112-119">[118]</a></sup> Artistic finds are scarce; in some parts of Patagonia ceramics were never made, only being introduced by contact with Europeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELavallée1995187_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELavallée1995187-120">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceania">Oceania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Oceania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australian_art" title="Indigenous Australian art">Indigenous Australian art</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yankee_Hat_art-MJC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Yankee_Hat_art-MJC.jpg/200px-Yankee_Hat_art-MJC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Yankee_Hat_art-MJC.jpg/300px-Yankee_Hat_art-MJC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Yankee_Hat_art-MJC.jpg/400px-Yankee_Hat_art-MJC.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yankee_Hat_art-MJC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>rock painting in the <a href="/wiki/Namadgi_National_Park" title="Namadgi National Park">Namadgi National Park</a></div></div></div>
<p>From earliest times <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aboriginal_and_Torres_Strait_Islander" title="Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islander">Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islanders</a> have been creating distinctive patterns of art. Much of the art is transitory, drawn in sand or on the human body to illustrate a place, a totem, or a cultural story. Early surviving artworks are mostly rock paintings. Some are called <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> paintings because they show the bones and organs of the animals they depict. Some Aboriginal art appears as <a href="/wiki/Abstract_art" title="Abstract art">abstract</a> to modern viewers; Aboriginal art employs geometrical figures, dots and lines to present the story being told.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bradshaw_rock_paintings.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bradshaw_rock_paintings.jpg/240px-Bradshaw_rock_paintings.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bradshaw_rock_paintings.jpg/360px-Bradshaw_rock_paintings.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bradshaw_rock_paintings.jpg/480px-Bradshaw_rock_paintings.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bradshaw_rock_paintings.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gwion_Gwion_rock_art" title="Gwion Gwion rock art">Gwion Gwion rock art</a> found in the north-west <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kimberley_region_of_Western_Australia" title="Kimberley region of Western Australia">Kimberley region of Western Australia</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gwion_Gwion_rock_art" title="Gwion Gwion rock art">Gwion Gwion rock art</a> are one of many styles of rock art found in Western Australia. They are predominantly human figures drawn in fine detail with accurate anatomical proportioning. They are usually dated to be at least 17,000 years old, and there have been suggestions they are as much as 70,000 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sydney_rock_engravings" title="Sydney rock engravings">Sydney rock engravings</a> are also a prominent rock art site in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Museum_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Museum-122">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polynesia">Polynesia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Polynesia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The natives of <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesia</a> have a distinct artistic heritage. While many of their artifacts were made with organic materials and thus lost to history, some of their most striking achievements survive in clay and stone. Among these are numerous pottery fragments from western <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a>, from the late 2nd millennium BCE. Also, the natives of Polynesia left scattered around their islands <a href="/wiki/Petroglyph" title="Petroglyph">Petroglyphs</a>, stone platforms or <a href="/wiki/Marae" title="Marae">Marae</a>, and sculptures of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ancestor_figure&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ancestor figure (page does not exist)">ancestor figures</a>, the most famous of which are the <a href="/wiki/Moai" title="Moai">Moai</a> of <a href="/wiki/Easter_Island" title="Easter Island">Easter Island</a>.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Prehistoric_art&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
